###xml 83 92 83 92 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">Wolbachia</italic>
###xml 287 298 287 298 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N. risticii</italic>
###xml 303 314 303 314 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N. sennetsu</italic>
###xml 412 420 412 420 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="F4">Figure 4</xref>
###xml 698 706 698 706 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="F4">Figure 4</xref>
###xml 18 29 <span type="species:ncbi:950">N. risticii</span>
###xml 31 42 <span type="species:ncbi:951">N. sennetsu</span>
###xml 44 58 <span type="species:ncbi:945">E. chaffeensis</span>
###xml 60 78 <span type="species:ncbi:948">A. phagocytophilum</span>
###xml 287 298 <span type="species:ncbi:950">N. risticii</span>
###xml 303 314 <span type="species:ncbi:951">N. sennetsu</span>
As the genomes of N. risticii, N. sennetsu, E. chaffeensis, A. phagocytophilum and Wolbachia wBm have been annotated and assigned role categories in an identical manner, we compared the number of genes pertaining to different functional roles. This comparison demonstrates that although N. risticii and N. sennetsu encode fewer proteins, they possess similar numbers of genes involved in housekeeping functions (Figure 4C). The only reduction of gene number occurs in the category of proteins with unknown functions, suggesting that essential housekeeping genes cannot be sacrificed as the genome reduces, including genes encoding metabolism, protein synthesis, transport and regulatory functions (Figure 4C).
###end p 47
###begin title 48
Metabolism
###end title 48
###begin title 49
Central metabolic pathways and transporters
###end title 49
###begin p 50
###xml 29 40 29 40 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N. risticii</italic>
###xml 108 116 108 116 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">in vitro</italic>
###xml 118 120 118 120 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B34">34</xref>
###xml 139 150 139 150 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N. risticii</italic>
###xml 313 314 313 314 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">0</sub>
###xml 315 316 315 316 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">1</sub>
###xml 443 464 443 464 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">Rickettsia prowazekii</italic>
###xml 469 479 469 479 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">R. conorii</italic>
###xml 481 483 481 483 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B35">35</xref>
###xml 486 497 486 497 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N. risticii</italic>
###xml 530 535 530 535 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">cydAB</italic>
###xml 656 667 656 667 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N. risticii</italic>
###xml 846 857 846 857 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N. risticii</italic>
###xml 1292 1303 1292 1303 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N. risticii</italic>
###xml 1481 1492 1481 1492 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N. risticii</italic>
###xml 1635 1637 1635 1637 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B36">36</xref>
###xml 1638 1640 1638 1640 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B37">37</xref>
###xml 1650 1660 1650 1660 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">Rickettsia</italic>
###xml 1667 1678 1667 1678 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N. risticii</italic>
###xml 29 40 <span type="species:ncbi:950">N. risticii</span>
###xml 139 150 <span type="species:ncbi:950">N. risticii</span>
###xml 443 464 <span type="species:ncbi:782">Rickettsia prowazekii</span>
###xml 469 479 <span type="species:ncbi:781">R. conorii</span>
###xml 486 497 <span type="species:ncbi:950">N. risticii</span>
###xml 656 667 <span type="species:ncbi:950">N. risticii</span>
###xml 846 857 <span type="species:ncbi:950">N. risticii</span>
###xml 1292 1303 <span type="species:ncbi:950">N. risticii</span>
###xml 1481 1492 <span type="species:ncbi:950">N. risticii</span>
###xml 1667 1678 <span type="species:ncbi:950">N. risticii</span>
A previous study showed that N. risticii is an aerobic organism and capable of ATP synthesis from glutamine in vitro (34). Analysis of the N. risticii genome suggests that it encodes pathways for aerobic respiration, including pyruvate metabolism, the tricarboxylic-acid cycle, and the electron transport chain (F0F1-ATPase, NADH dehydrogenase, succinate dehydrogenase, cytochrome reductase, and cytochrome oxidase complexes). However, unlike Rickettsia prowazekii and R. conorii (35), N. risticii does not encode d-type oxidase (cydAB, cytochrome d ubiquinol oxidase), which is expressed during low oxygen conditions and has a higher affinity for oxygen. N. risticii is unable to use glucose, fructose, or fatty acids directly as a carbon or energy source, since essential enzymes for the utilization of these substrates are not identified. The N. risticii genome only encodes a partial glycolysis pathway, which lacks hexokinases and is thus unable to convert glucose to glucose-6-phosphate, and a partial gluconeogenesis pathway, which terminates at fructose-6-phosphate. The enzymes in the glycolysis pathway are limited to those that produce glyceraldehydes-3-phosphate and dihydroxyacetone phosphate from phosphoenolpyruvate. A complete nonoxidative pentose-phosphate pathway exists in N. risticii, which utilizes glyceraldehydes-3-phosphate to produce pentose that is needed for nucleotide and cofactor biosynthesis. To compensate for the absent or incomplete pathways, the N. risticii genome contains several orthologs involved in membrane transport systems that can supply the necessary amino acids, metabolites, and ions () (36,37). Unlike Rickettsia spp., N. risticii does not encode translocases for ATP or NADH (ATP:ADP antiporter family), so it appears to rely on its own intracellular ATP production or have a unique ATP acquisition mechanism ().
###end p 50
###begin title 51
Nucleotide, cofactor and amino-acid biosynthesis
###end title 51
###begin p 52
###xml 32 34 32 34 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B17">17</xref>
###xml 37 48 37 48 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N. risticii</italic>
###xml 209 220 209 220 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N. risticii</italic>
###xml 358 369 358 369 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N. risticii</italic>
###xml 478 489 478 489 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N. risticii</italic>
###xml 575 577 575 577 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B38">38</xref>
###xml 37 48 <span type="species:ncbi:950">N. risticii</span>
###xml 209 220 <span type="species:ncbi:950">N. risticii</span>
###xml 358 369 <span type="species:ncbi:950">N. risticii</span>
###xml 478 489 <span type="species:ncbi:950">N. risticii</span>
Like all other Anaplasmataceae (17), N. risticii is limited to synthesizing only a few amino acids, such as aspartate, glycine and glutamine and thus must transport most amino acids from its host (). However, N. risticii is capable of synthesizing all nucleotides and most vitamins or cofactors, such as biotin, folate, FAD, NAD, CoA and protoheme (). Since N. risticii is maintained transstadially and transovarially inside its invertebrate trematode host, it is possible that N. risticii is beneficial to the host by providing necessary vitamins and cofactors (mutualism) (38).
###end p 52
###begin title 53
Information transfer and DNA repair
###end title 53
###begin p 54
###xml 259 270 259 270 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N. risticii</italic>
###xml 347 351 347 351 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">rnpB</italic>
###xml 509 520 509 520 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N. sennetsu</italic>
###xml 630 632 630 632 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B17">17</xref>
###xml 646 657 646 657 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N. risticii</italic>
###xml 666 670 666 670 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">uvrD</italic>
###xml 826 829 826 829 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">3mg</italic>
###xml 834 837 834 837 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">fpg</italic>
###xml 901 907 901 907 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">uvrABC</italic>
###xml 919 930 919 930 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N. risticii</italic>
###xml 1237 1250 1233 1246 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">Neorickettsia</italic>
###xml 1287 1295 1283 1291 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="F1">Figure 1</xref>
###xml 259 270 <span type="species:ncbi:950">N. risticii</span>
###xml 509 520 <span type="species:ncbi:951">N. sennetsu</span>
###xml 646 657 <span type="species:ncbi:950">N. risticii</span>
###xml 919 930 <span type="species:ncbi:950">N. risticii</span>
Genes-encoding enzymes for DNA replication and repair, RNA synthesis and degradation, ribosomal proteins (except L30 and S22), as well as genes involved in homologous recombination, including RecA/RecF pathways and RuvABC complexes, are all identified in the N. risticii genome. In addition, two small RNAs are also found in the genome, including rnpB (RNase P RNA component precursor) and tmRNA (responsible for tagging incomplete proteins for proteolysis on stalled ribosomes). A previous study showed that N. sennetsu encodes the most limited genes for DNA repair among several sequenced members of the family Anaplasmataceae (17). Similarly, N. risticii encodes uvrD family genes, but lacks most genes required for the repair of UV-induced DNA damage, including various glycosylases for base excision repair (BER, such as 3mg and fpg) and exonucleases for nucleotide excision repair (NER, such as uvrABC). Instead, N. risticii might have gained limited DNA repair function by the acquisition of a gene (NRI_0805) most closely related to DNA photolyases of gamma-proteobacteria, which is an alternative mechanism to repair UV-damaged DNA (). The absence of many DNA repair pathways might account for the longer evolutionary branch of Neorickettsia spp. in the family Anaplasmataceae (Figure 1).
###end p 54
###begin p 55
###xml 41 52 41 52 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N. risticii</italic>
###xml 91 102 91 102 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N. sennetsu</italic>
###xml 180 188 180 188 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="F3">Figure 3</xref>
###xml 305 307 305 307 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B39">39</xref>
###xml 344 358 344 358 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">E. chaffeensis</italic>
###xml 363 371 363 371 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">E. canis</italic>
###xml 376 394 376 394 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">A. phagocytophilum</italic>
###xml 399 411 399 411 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">A. marginale</italic>
###xml 467 478 467 478 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N. risticii</italic>
###xml 483 494 483 494 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N. sennetsu</italic>
###xml 496 504 496 504 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="F1">Figure 1</xref>
###xml 561 563 561 563 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B40">40</xref>
###xml 702 715 702 715 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">Neorickettsia</italic>
###xml 953 966 953 966 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">Neorickettsia</italic>
###xml 1180 1190 1180 1190 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">Salmonella</italic>
###xml 1195 1211 1195 1211 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">Escherichia coli</italic>
###xml 1213 1215 1213 1215 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B41">41</xref>
###xml 1262 1275 1262 1275 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">Neorickettsia</italic>
###xml 41 52 <span type="species:ncbi:950">N. risticii</span>
###xml 91 102 <span type="species:ncbi:951">N. sennetsu</span>
###xml 344 358 <span type="species:ncbi:945">E. chaffeensis</span>
###xml 363 371 <span type="species:ncbi:944|species:ncbi:507819|species:ncbi:214095">E. canis</span>
###xml 376 394 <span type="species:ncbi:948">A. phagocytophilum</span>
###xml 399 411 <span type="species:ncbi:770">A. marginale</span>
###xml 467 478 <span type="species:ncbi:950">N. risticii</span>
###xml 483 494 <span type="species:ncbi:951">N. sennetsu</span>
###xml 1195 1211 <span type="species:ncbi:562">Escherichia coli</span>
Despite the distinct trematode hosts for N. risticii (which reside in aquatic insects) and N. sennetsu (which supposedly reside in fish), there is almost complete genomic synteny (Figure 3). It is assumed that, on average, the number of genome rearrangements increases with time (or sequence divergence) (39). However, synteny analysis between E. chaffeensis and E. canis, or A. phagocytophilum and A. marginale, both of which have a small phylogenetic divergence as N. risticii and N. sennetsu (Figure 1), showed a single symmetrical inversion in the genomes (40). This suggests that there have been very few rearrangement events in these genomes, probably because large rearrangements are lethal for Neorickettsia spp. due to minimum sets of mutation repair genes. On the other hand, this poor mutation repair system allows accumulation of point mutations, resulting in new genes with potential novel functions. As housekeeping genes are essential to Neorickettsia spp., only those mutations that do not affect housekeeping functions accumulate. A poor mutation repair system due to the loss of mutation repair genes is an important virulence factor of common pathogens such as Salmonella and Escherichia coli (41). Therefore, the limited DNA repair system of Neorickettsia spp. may provide species survival advantage by allowing the accumulation of permissive mutations, thus enhancing genetic diversity in the stable intra-trematode environment where events leading to genomic rearrangements may be rare.
###end p 55
###begin title 56
Pathogenesis
###end title 56
###begin p 57
###xml 60 71 60 71 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N. risticii</italic>
###xml 174 185 174 185 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N. risticii</italic>
###xml 306 317 306 317 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N. risticii</italic>
###xml 60 71 <span type="species:ncbi:950">N. risticii</span>
###xml 174 185 <span type="species:ncbi:950">N. risticii</span>
###xml 306 317 <span type="species:ncbi:950">N. risticii</span>
Little is known about the genetic determinants required for N. risticii to invade hosts and cause disease. Here, we identify several putative genes that might be involved in N. risticii pathogenesis, including outer membrane proteins, protein secretion systems, two-component regulatory systems, and other N. risticii-specific genes.
###end p 57
###begin title 58
Protein secretion systems
###end title 58
###begin p 59
###xml 434 436 434 436 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B42">42</xref>
###xml 455 466 455 466 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N. risticii</italic>
###xml 684 695 684 695 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N. risticii</italic>
###xml 704 709 704 709 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B43 B44 B45">43&#8211;45</xref>
###xml 1234 1245 1234 1245 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N. risticii</italic>
###xml 455 466 <span type="species:ncbi:950">N. risticii</span>
###xml 684 695 <span type="species:ncbi:950">N. risticii</span>
###xml 1234 1245 <span type="species:ncbi:950">N. risticii</span>
Extracellular secretion of products is the major mechanism by which pathogenic Gram-negative bacteria alter host cell functions, thus enhancing survival of the bacteria and/or damaging hosts. In order to secrete virulence factors across two lipid bilayers and the periplasm in between, Gram-negative pathogens use at least six distinct extracellular protein secretion systems, referred to as type I-VI secretion systems or T1SS-T6SS (42). Analysis of the N. risticii genome shows that it encodes both Sec-dependent and Sec-independent protein export pathways for secretion of proteins across the membranes, but no homologs of T2SS, T3SS, T5SS or T6SS components could be found in the N. risticii genome (43-45). All major components for the Sec-dependent pathway are identified, including the cytosolic protein-export chaperone SecB and the preprotein translocase complex SecY/SecG/SecA/SecD/YajC. The Sec-independent T1SS, an ATP-driven ABC transporter system, is capable of transporting target proteins carrying a C-terminal secretion signal across both inner and outer membranes and into the extracellular medium. Both components of the T1SS, the ATPase AprD and the membrane fusion HlyD family protein AprE, are identified in the N. risticii genome.
###end p 59
###begin p 60
###xml 215 217 215 217 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B46">46</xref>
###xml 218 220 218 220 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B47">47</xref>
###xml 304 315 304 315 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N. risticii</italic>
###xml 410 421 410 421 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N. risticii</italic>
###xml 514 525 514 525 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N. risticii</italic>
###xml 612 623 612 623 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N. risticii</italic>
###xml 642 647 642 647 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">groEL</italic>
###xml 649 654 649 654 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">groES</italic>
###xml 656 660 656 660 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">dnaK</italic>
###xml 662 666 662 666 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">dnaJ</italic>
###xml 668 672 668 672 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">hscB</italic>
###xml 677 681 677 681 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">htpG</italic>
###xml 304 315 <span type="species:ncbi:950">N. risticii</span>
###xml 410 421 <span type="species:ncbi:950">N. risticii</span>
###xml 514 525 <span type="species:ncbi:950">N. risticii</span>
###xml 612 623 <span type="species:ncbi:950">N. risticii</span>
The twin-arginine dependent translocation (Tat) pathway can transport folded proteins across the bacterial cytoplasmic membrane by recognizing N-terminal signal peptides harboring a distinctive twin-arginine motif (46,47). Components of the Tat pathway including TatA and TatC are also identified in the N. risticii genome. Using the TATFIND algorithm (), prokaryotic Tat signal peptides are identified in two N. risticii proteins (NRI_0595 and NRI_0661). Nevertheless, the presence of a functional Tat pathway in N. risticii remains to be studied. In addition, a set of common chaperonins is also identified in N. risticii genome, including groEL, groES, dnaK, dnaJ, hscB and htpG.
###end p 60
###begin p 61
###xml 222 224 222 224 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B48">48</xref>
###xml 528 530 528 530 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B23">23</xref>
###xml 531 533 531 533 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B49">49</xref>
###xml 534 536 534 536 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B50">50</xref>

###xml 2892 2922 <span type="species:ncbi:272947">Rickettsia prowazekii Madrid E</span>
###xml 2983 3010 <span type="species:ncbi:272944">Rickettsia conorii Malish 7</span>
###xml 3072 3099 <span type="species:ncbi:190650">Caulobacter crescentus CB15</span>
###xml 3218 3229 <span type="species:ncbi:950">N. risticii</span>
###xml 3234 3245 <span type="species:ncbi:951">N. sennetsu</span>
###xml 3298 3312 <span type="species:ncbi:945">E. chaffeensis</span>
###xml 3317 3335 <span type="species:ncbi:948">A. phagocytophilum</span>
The T4SS is a protein secretion system that can translocate bacterial effector molecules into host cells and plays a key role in pathogen-host cell interactions, usually involved in pathogenesis of Gram-negative bacteria (48). In several intracellular bacteria including the family Anaplasmataceae, the T4SS is essential for survival inside host cells, involved in nutrient acquisition, inhibition of phago-lysosomal fusion pathways, and most importantly, establishment of intracellular compartments and intracellular survival (23,49,50). In the N. risticii genome, we also identified a T4SS encoded by virB/D genes distributed in four separate loci: virB8-2/9-2, virB8-1/virB9-1/virB10/virB11/virD4, virB2-1/virB2-2/virB4-2 and virB3/virB4-1/virB6-1/virB6-2/virB6-3/virB6-4 (Figure 5A). In addition, there are a number of differences. Unlike A. phagocytophilum and E. chaffeensis (51), the sodB gene is not present upstream of the virB3/virB4/virB6 operon in Neorickettsia spp., and virB8-2/9-2 are organized into a cluster. Furthermore, N. risticii encodes a much shorter virB6-4 (approximately1000 aa) compared to those of other Anaplasmataceae (>2000 aa). Analysis of N. risticii and N. sennetsu genome sequences shows that they both encode two copies of virB2 downstream of virB4 (Figure 5). Phylogenic analysis shows that virB2 genes of N. risticii and N. sennetsu are closely related to virB2 genes of other alpha-proteobacteria, but are phylogenetically distinct from virB2 genes of E. chaffeensis and A. phagocytophilum, which form a separate clade (Figure 5B) (32). Figure 5.Genomic organization of T4SS virB/D clusters and phylogenic analysis of virB2 genes in the family Anaplasmataceae. (A) Circular representation of T4SS virB/D genes in genomes of the family Anaplasmataceae. From outside to inside circles: first (black)-N. risticii (879 977 bp); second (red)-N. sennetsu (859 006 bp); third (blue)-A. phagocytophilum (1 471 282 bp); fourth (green)-E. chaffeensis (1 176 248 bp). The individual virB/D genes were color-coded in the clusters for better visualization. Note that all T4SS genes of N. risticii and N. sennetsu, and most T4SS genes of A. phagocytophilum and E. chaffeensis are encoded in the '-' strand, therefore, the location of most genes appear close to the inner circles. Genes encoded in the '+' strand are followed by red arrows. (B) Phylogenetic tree of virB2 genes in the family Anaplasmataceae. Nucleotide sequences of virB2 from members of the family Anaplasmataceae were aligned using the Clustal W method, and a phylogenetic tree was built. APHxxxx: locus ids for virB2 genes of A. phagocytophilum HZ; ECHxxxx: locus ids of E. chaffeensis Arkansas; NSExxxx: locus ids of N. sennetsu Miyayama; NRIxxxx: locus ids of N. risticii Illinois; ATU6168: Agrobacterium tumefaciens C58 pilin subunit, TrbC/VirB2 family protein (Accession No. NP_396488); RP192: Rickettsia prowazekii Madrid E TrbC/VirB2 family protein (Accession No. NP_359878); RC241: Rickettsia conorii Malish 7 TrbC/VirB2 family protein (Accession No. NP_359878); CC2417: Caulobacter crescentus CB15 TrbC/VirB2 family protein (Accession No. NP_421220).A). The organization of virB/D gene clusters is conserved between N. risticii and N. sennetsu, but is shifted and/or inverted relative to that of E. chaffeensis and A. phagocytophilum (Figure 5

DNase-treated total RNA was reverse-transcribed (RT+) and subsequently PCR-amplified using primers specific to <italic>virB2</italic> genes: NRI_0738 and NRI_0740. The right panel is a 5&#215; amplification of the inlet from the merged image. Results are representative of three independent experiments. Note average bipolar localization of VirB2 per bacterium on the surface of <italic>N. risticii</italic> and variable sizes of bacteria.</p>

###xml 302 313 <span type="species:ncbi:950">N. risticii</span>
###xml 471 482 <span type="species:ncbi:950">N. risticii</span>
###xml 568 579 <span type="species:ncbi:950">N. risticii</span>
###xml 680 691 <span type="species:ncbi:950">N. risticii</span>
###xml 785 796 <span type="species:ncbi:950">N. risticii</span>
###xml 933 944 <span type="species:ncbi:950">N. risticii</span>
###xml 1000 1011 <span type="species:ncbi:950">N. risticii</span>
###xml 1517 1528 <span type="species:ncbi:950">N. risticii</span>
###xml 1636 1647 <span type="species:ncbi:950">N. risticii</span>
###xml 1776 1787 <span type="species:ncbi:950">N. risticii</span>
###xml 1891 1896 <span type="species:ncbi:9796">horse</span>
###xml 1902 1913 <span type="species:ncbi:950">N. risticii</span>
###xml 1915 1918 <span type="species:ncbi:950">NRI</span>
###xml 1924 1930 <span type="species:ncbi:9986">rabbit</span>
###xml 2120 2131 <span type="species:ncbi:950">N. risticii</span>
###xml 2216 2221 <span type="species:ncbi:9796">horse</span>
###xml 2227 2238 <span type="species:ncbi:950">N. risticii</span>
###xml 2240 2243 <span type="species:ncbi:950">NRI</span>
###xml 2249 2255 <span type="species:ncbi:9986">rabbit</span>
###xml 2489 2500 <span type="species:ncbi:950">N. risticii</span>
Subcellular fractionation and functional studies have demonstrated that VirB2 is the major pilus component of T4SS extracellular filaments and may play a critical role in mediating the initial interaction with host cells (48,49). RT-PCR and western blotting results indicate that VirB2 is expressed by N. risticii in infected P388D1 cells, and two virB2 genes can be co-transcribed as an operon (Figure 6A and B). Double immunofluorescence assays from both intracellular N. risticii organisms in permeabilized infected cells (Figure 6C) and non-permeabilized purified N. risticii organisms show that VirB2 forms a punctate pattern, mostly localized at two poles on the surface of N. risticii (Figure 6D). These results suggest that VirB2 can form focal complexes on the surface of the N. risticii organism, which might serve as channels of the T4SS apparatus, like that of Agrobacterium (48). Figure 6.Expression and localization of N. risticii VirB2 (NRI_0738/NRI_0740). (A) RNAs were prepared from N. risticii-infected P388D1 cells at 3 days p.i. DNase-treated total RNA was reverse-transcribed (RT+) and subsequently PCR-amplified using primers specific to virB2 genes: NRI_0738 and NRI_0740. RT-: negative control without reverse transcriptase; 738: PCR with primers targeting NRI_0738 only; 740: PCR with primers targeting NRI_0740 only; 738 + 740: PCR with primers spanning NRI_0738 and NRI_0740. The relative sizes of molecular mass standards are shown (in base pairs) on the left. (B) Whole-cell lysates from N. risticii-infected P388D1 cells (3 days p.i.) were prepared and subjected to western blotting using antibody against N. risticii VirB2 (NRI_0738), or host alpha-tubulin as a loading control. The molecular mass standards are shown (in kDa) on the right. (C) N. risticii-infected P388D1 cells at 3 days p.i. were fixed, permeabilized with 0.1% saponin and dual labeled with horse anti-N. risticii (NRI) and rabbit anti-VirB2-1 (NRI_0738) antibodies. Results are representative of three independent experiments. Note bright dots of VirB2 on most of intracellular bacteria. Bar: 5 mum. (D) Host cell-free N. risticii purified from infected P388D1 cells at 3 days p.i. were fixed and dual labeled with horse anti-N. risticii (NRI) and rabbit anti-VirB2 antibodies. The right panel is a 5x amplification of the inlet from the merged image. Results are representative of three independent experiments. Note average bipolar localization of VirB2 per bacterium on the surface of N. risticii and variable sizes of bacteria.
###end p 63

xlink:href="http://genomics.ornl.gov/mist">http://genomics.ornl.gov/mist</ext-link>).</p></fn><fn id="TF13"><p textid="73"><sup>e</sup>Location of outer member proteins is predicted by the pSort-B algorithm.</p></fn></table-wrap-foot></table-wrap>
###xml 545 559 <span type="species:ncbi:945">E. chaffeensis</span>
###xml 564 582 <span type="species:ncbi:948">A. phagocytophilum</span>
###xml 786 797 <span type="species:ncbi:950">N. risticii</span>
###xml 1307 1318 <span type="species:ncbi:950">N. risticii</span>
###xml 2157 2175 <span type="species:ncbi:948">A. phagocytophilum</span>
###xml 2182 2196 <span type="species:ncbi:945">E. chaffeensis</span>
###xml 2198 2201 <span type="species:ncbi:951">NSE</span>
###xml 2203 2214 <span type="species:ncbi:951">N. sennetsu</span>
###xml 2216 2219 <span type="species:ncbi:950">NRI</span>
###xml 2221 2232 <span type="species:ncbi:950">N. risticii</span>
###xml 2243 2282 <span type="species:ncbi:80849">Wolbachia endosymbiont of Brugia malayi</span>
###xml 2494 2505 <span type="species:ncbi:951">N. sennetsu</span>
###xml 2563 2577 <span type="species:ncbi:945">E. chaffeensis</span>
###xml 2581 2599 <span type="species:ncbi:948">A. phagocytophilum</span>
A two-component regulatory system (TCS) is a signal transduction system that allows bacteria to sense and respond rapidly to changes in their environment. Through the regulation of downstream gene expression, the TCS plays a key role in controlling virulence responses of a wide variety of bacterial pathogens (52). A TCS is generally composed of a sensor histidine kinase and a cognate response regulator, which is often a transcription factor and controls gene expression in response to environmental changes. Our previous studies showed that E. chaffeensis and A. phagocytophilum express three pairs of TCS, including CckA/CtrA, PleC/PleD and NtrX/NtrY and that their histidine kinase activities were required for bacterial infection (53,54). Computational analysis reveals that the N. risticii genome only encodes two pairs of TCS: CckA/CtrA and PleC/PleD (Table 4). The response regulator CtrA is a global transcription factor found only in alpha-proteobacteria that coordinates multiple cell cycle events at the transcriptional level. The other regulator PleD contains a GGDEF domain, which is associated with diguanyl cyclase activity that generates cyclic diguanylate (c-di-GMP) to regulate biofilm or extracellular matrix formation (55). ###begin p 74
###xml 211 213 211 213 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B56">56</xref>
###xml 214 216 214 216 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B57">57</xref>
###xml 348 350 348 350 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B56">56</xref>
###xml 430 441 430 441 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N. risticii</italic>
###xml 537 544 537 544 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="table" rid="T4">Table 4</xref>
###xml 693 704 693 704 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N. risticii</italic>
###xml 706 708 706 708 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B56">56</xref>
###xml 430 441 <span type="species:ncbi:950">N. risticii</span>
###xml 693 704 <span type="species:ncbi:950">N. risticii</span>
Prokaryotes also carry out signaling events using the one-component regulatory system, which consists of a single protein containing both input and output domains, but lacks the phospho-transfer domains of TCS (56,57). The number of one-component systems and TCS per genome positively correlates with the genome size and the total number of genes (56). Comparison among members of the family Anaplasmataceae showed that, although N. risticii has the smallest genome size, it encodes the highest numbers of one-component system proteins (Table 4 and ). The disproportionate increase in the number of one-component systems might result from the complex lifecycle and environmental conditions of N. risticii (56).
###end p 74
###begin p 75
###xml 31 42 31 42 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N. risticii</italic>
###xml 109 116 109 116 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="table" rid="T4">Table 4</xref>
###xml 204 206 204 206 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B57">57</xref>
###xml 380 398 380 398 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">A. phagocytophilum</italic>
###xml 403 417 403 417 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">E. chaffeensis</italic>
###xml 419 421 419 421 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B58">58</xref>
###xml 422 424 422 424 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B59">59</xref>
###xml 31 42 <span type="species:ncbi:950">N. risticii</span>
###xml 380 398 <span type="species:ncbi:948">A. phagocytophilum</span>
###xml 403 417 <span type="species:ncbi:945">E. chaffeensis</span>
Interestingly, analysis of the N. risticii genome shows that it encodes two copies of PleC histidine kinase (Table 4) and a one-component signal transduction protein, an EAL domain protein (NRI_0417) () (57). An EAL domain protein has diguanylate phosphodiesterase (PDE) activity and can function as a c-di-GMP PDE that converts c-di-GMP to GMP. EAL domain proteins are absent in A. phagocytophilum and E. chaffeensis (58,59).
###end p 75
###begin title 76
Transcriptional regulations
###end title 76
###begin p 77
###xml 417 428 417 428 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N. risticii</italic>
###xml 872 890 872 890 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">A. phagocytophilum</italic>
###xml 895 909 895 909 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">E. chaffeensis</italic>
###xml 945 949 945 949 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ApxR</italic>
###xml 954 958 954 958 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">EcxR</italic>
###xml 1065 1070 1065 1070 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B60 B61 B62">60&#8211;62</xref>
###xml 1114 1125 1114 1125 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N. risticii</italic>
###xml 1153 1157 1153 1157 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">NrxR</italic>
###xml 417 428 <span type="species:ncbi:950">N. risticii</span>
###xml 872 890 <span type="species:ncbi:948">A. phagocytophilum</span>
###xml 895 909 <span type="species:ncbi:945">E. chaffeensis</span>
###xml 1114 1125 <span type="species:ncbi:950">N. risticii</span>
Members in the order Rickettsiales have a small number of transcriptional regulators; a trait also observed in other intracellular pathogens. This phenomenon appears to be the result of reductive evolution coupled with the diminished demand for regulation due to the small genome size, as well as the relatively stable conditions provided by the intracellular environment of the host cells. Like all Anaplasmataceae, N. risticii encodes only two sigma factors: RNA polymerase sigma-70 factor (RpoD), which is the essential primary sigma factor and responsible for most RNA synthesis in exponentially growing cells, and sigma-32 factor (rpoH), which is responsible for expression from heat shock promoters. In addition, the response regulator CtrA might regulate the expression of genes involved in the bacterial developmental cycle. Our previous studies demonstrated that A. phagocytophilum and E. chaffeensis encode a transcriptional regulator ApxR and EcxR, respectively, which regulates the expression of genes encoding P44 outer membrane proteins and the T4SS (60-62). Based on sequence homology, the ortholog N. risticii transcriptional regulator (NrxR, NRI_0036), which encodes a 12.5-kDa DNA binding protein, is identified.
###end p 77
###begin title 78
Ankyrin domain proteins
###end title 78
###begin p 79
###xml 213 231 213 231 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">A. phagocytophilum</italic>
###xml 407 409 407 409 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B23">23</xref>
###xml 485 496 485 496 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N. risticii</italic>
###xml 213 231 <span type="species:ncbi:948">A. phagocytophilum</span>
###xml 485 496 <span type="species:ncbi:950">N. risticii</span>
Ankyrin repeats have been found in numerous proteins mediating specific protein-protein interactions in bacteria and eukaryotes. Our previous study has suggested that the ankyrin repeat-containing protein AnkA of A. phagocytophilum is secreted into host cells by the T4SS and plays an important role in facilitating intracellular infection by activating the Abl-1 protein tyrosine kinase signaling pathway (23). Three proteins that contain ankyrin-repeat domains are identified in the N. risticii genome. However, whether any of the ankyrin repeat-containing proteins can be secreted by the T4SS and regulate host-cell signaling remains to be studied.
###end p 79
###begin title 80
Cell-wall components
###end title 80
###begin title 81
Lipopolysaccharide and peptidoglycan
###end title 81
###begin p 82
###xml 142 144 142 144 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B17">17</xref>
###xml 145 147 145 147 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B63">63</xref>
###xml 154 165 154 165 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N. risticii</italic>
###xml 459 470 459 470 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N. risticii</italic>
###xml 666 668 666 668 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B64">64</xref>
###xml 669 671 669 671 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B65">65</xref>
###xml 789 800 789 800 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N. risticii</italic>
###xml 154 165 <span type="species:ncbi:950">N. risticii</span>
###xml 459 470 <span type="species:ncbi:950">N. risticii</span>
###xml 789 800 <span type="species:ncbi:950">N. risticii</span>
Most of the genes encoding peptidoglycan and lipopolysaccharide (LPS), including lipid A are absent in members of the family Anaplasmataceae (17,63). The N. risticii genome does not encode any enzymes involved in the biosynthesis of lipid A (the core component of LPS) and lacks most enzymes required for the biosynthesis and degradation of diaminopimelate and murein sacculus (components of peptidoglycan) indicating that LPS and peptidoglycan are absent in N. risticii. Since conserved pathogen-associated molecular patterns like LPS or peptidoglycan are potent stimulants for innate immunity and anti-microbial responses in mammalian host immune defensive cells (64,65), the loss of LPS and peptidoglycan eliminates these microbicidal activities in leukocytes and enhances survival for N. risticii in mammalian monocytes/macrophages.
###end p 82
###begin title 83
Lipoproteins
###end title 83
###begin p 84
###xml 28 42 28 42 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">E. chaffeensis</italic>
###xml 89 91 89 91 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B66">66</xref>
###xml 481 482 481 482 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N</italic>
###xml 592 594 592 594 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B67">67</xref>
###xml 595 597 595 597 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B68">68</xref>
###xml 659 670 659 670 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N. risticii</italic>
###xml 672 675 672 675 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">lgt</italic>
###xml 688 692 688 692 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">lspA</italic>
###xml 709 712 709 712 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">lnt</italic>
###xml 938 940 938 940 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B69">69</xref>
###xml 1043 1057 1043 1057 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">E. chaffeensis</italic>
###xml 1131 1133 1131 1133 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B66">66</xref>
###xml 1154 1165 1154 1165 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N. risticii</italic>
###xml 28 42 <span type="species:ncbi:945">E. chaffeensis</span>
###xml 659 670 <span type="species:ncbi:950">N. risticii</span>
###xml 1043 1057 <span type="species:ncbi:945">E. chaffeensis</span>
###xml 1125 1129 <span type="species:ncbi:9615">dogs</span>
###xml 1154 1165 <span type="species:ncbi:950">N. risticii</span>
###xml 1245 1251 <span type="species:ncbi:9796">horses</span>
Studies have suggested that E. chaffeensis is capable of expressing mature lipoproteins (66), which involves three lipoprotein-processing enzymes: a prolipoprotein diacylglyceryl transferase (Lgt) that attaches the thiol group of the conserved cysteine residue in the lipobox with a diacylglyceryl group, a lipoprotein-processing protease signal peptidase II (SPase II, LspA) that cleaves prolipoproteins before the conserved cysteine residue in the lipobox, and an apolipoprotein N-acyltransferase (Lnt) that adds lipids to the amine of the newly cleaved amino-terminal cysteine (cylation) (67,68). All three lipoprotein-processing enzymes are identified in N. risticii: lgt (NRI_0823), lspA (NRI_0879), and lnt (NRI_0470). Furthermore, thirteen lipoproteins are identified by computational prediction using the LipoP 1.0 program (), which is specific for the prediction of lipoproteins and signal peptides in Gram-negative bacteria () (69). Among these lipoproteins, nine are predicted to be located on the outer membrane. Given the role of E. chaffeensis lipoproteins in inducing delayed-type hypersensitivity reaction in dogs (66), it is likely that N. risticii lipoproteins are also involved in pathogenesis and immune response in infected horses.
###end p 84
###begin title 85
Putative outer membrane proteins
###end title 85
###begin p 86
###xml 283 285 283 285 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B70">70</xref>
###xml 288 295 288 295 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="table" rid="T4">Table 4</xref>
###xml 447 458 447 458 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N. risticii</italic>

###xml 1204 1215 <span type="species:ncbi:951">N. sennetsu</span>
###xml 1301 1312 <span type="species:ncbi:950">N. risticii</span>
###xml 1336 1347 <span type="species:ncbi:950">N. risticii</span>
###xml 1385 1396 <span type="species:ncbi:950">N. risticii</span>
Computational analysis with the pSort-B algorithm predicted nine outer membrane proteins, two of which (rotamase family protein and outer membrane protein assembly complex YaeT protein or Omp85 family protein) might be involved in outer membrane protein transport and stabilization (70) (Table 4 and ). Unlike most other members in the family Anaplasmataceae, which encode a diverse complement of OMP-1/MSP2/P44 outer member superfamily proteins, N. risticii encodes only one group of putative outer surface proteins that falls into this PFAM family (Pfam01617) (17). This group of proteins consists of three Wolbachia surface protein (WSP)-like N. risticii surface proteins, each approximately 30-kDa in mass (NSPs: NRI_0838, NRI_0839, and NRI_0841) (Figure 7A-C, and ). (B and E) Phylogenetic tree of nsp (B) and ssa (E) genes in N. risticii and N. sennetsu. Nucleotide sequences of nsp or ssa from N. risticii and N. sennetsu were aligned using the Clustal W method, and a phylogenetic tree was built. NRI25-D: N. risticii strain 25-D; NRI90-12: N. risticii strain 90-12. (C and F) Alignment of N. risticii NSP (C) and SSA (F) protein sequences using blast2seq.

###xml 293 295 293 295 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B72">72</xref>
###xml 321 330 321 330 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">Wolbachia</italic>
###xml 373 375 373 375 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B38">38</xref>

###xml 463 471 463 471 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="F7">Figure 7</xref>

###xml 600 613 600 613 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">Wolbachia wsp</italic>
###xml 616 631 616 631 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N. risticii nsp</italic>
###xml 637 652 637 652 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N. sennetsu nsp</italic>
###xml 721 723 721 723 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B17">17</xref>
###xml 726 734 726 734 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="F7">Figure 7</xref>
###xml 770 781 770 781 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N. risticii</italic>
###xml 940 948 940 948 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="F7">Figure 7</xref>
###xml 952 961 952 961 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">Wolbachia</italic>
###xml 1080 1085 1080 1085 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B73 B74 B75">73&#8211;75</xref>
###xml 1108 1119 1108 1119 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N. risticii</italic>
###xml 1165 1174 1165 1174 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">Wolbachia</italic>
###xml 1283 1301 1283 1301 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">A. phagocytophilum</italic>
###xml 1310 1324 1310 1324 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">E. chaffeensis</italic>
###xml 1448 1449 1448 1449 <sc xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">l</sc>
###xml 1520 1522 1520 1522 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B76">76</xref>
###xml 1523 1525 1523 1525 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B77">77</xref>
###xml 1707 1718 1704 1715 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N. risticii</italic>
###xml 205 217 <span type="species:ncbi:770">A. marginale</span>
###xml 378 389 <span type="species:ncbi:950">N. risticii</span>
###xml 616 627 <span type="species:ncbi:950">N. risticii</span>
###xml 637 648 <span type="species:ncbi:951">N. sennetsu</span>
###xml 770 781 <span type="species:ncbi:950">N. risticii</span>
###xml 996 1001 <span type="species:ncbi:9606">human</span>
###xml 1061 1066 <span type="species:ncbi:9606">human</span>
###xml 1108 1119 <span type="species:ncbi:950">N. risticii</span>
###xml 1283 1301 <span type="species:ncbi:948">A. phagocytophilum</span>
###xml 1310 1324 <span type="species:ncbi:945">E. chaffeensis</span>
###xml 1707 1718 <span type="species:ncbi:950">N. risticii</span>
WSPs have been identified as highly expressed proteins in Wolbachia strains and are likely surface-exposed, based on their similarities to other outer surface proteins in related bacteria (such as MSP4 in A. marginale) and through membrane fractionation and bacterial labeling experiments (71,72). Unlike WSP paralogs of Wolbachia strains that are scattered in the genome (38), N. risticii nsp genes (NRI_0838, NRI_0839 and NRI_0841) comprise a potential operon (Figure 7A). Alignment of Neorickettsia nsp and Wolbachia wsp genes shows that they are well conserved, with more than 35% identity among Wolbachia wsps, N. risticii nsps and N. sennetsu nsps (which were previously annotated to encode hypothetical proteins) (17) (Figure 7B). Furthermore, alignment of three N. risticii NSP proteins using blast2seq shows that NSP2 (NRI_0839) is related to NSP3 (NRI_0841), but NSP2 or NSP3 only share C-terminal fragments with NSP1 (NRI_0838) (Figure 7C). Wolbachia WSP proteins are known to inhibit human neutrophil apoptosis, and induce inflammatory responses in human neutrophils (73-75). It is possible that N. risticii NSP proteins can act in a similar fashion to Wolbachia WSP proteins, regulating host cell functions and immune responses. In addition, two members in this family, A. phagocytophilum P44 and E. chaffeensis P28/OMP-1F, have been demonstrated to possess porin activities of relatively large pore size to allow passive diffusion of l-glutamine, monosaccharides, disaccharides, and even tetrasaccharides (76,77). Given the similar predicted structures of amphipathic and antiparallel beta-strands, abundant polar residues, and a phenylalanine residue at the C-terminus of both NSP1 and NSP2, N. risticii NSP might also have porin activity to facilitate bacterial intracellular growth.
###end p 88
###begin p 89
###xml 30 41 30 41 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N. risticii</italic>
###xml 90 101 90 101 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N. risticii</italic>
###xml 300 311 300 311 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N. sennetsu</italic>
###xml 313 324 313 324 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N. risticii</italic>
###xml 329 353 329 353 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">Stellantchasmus falcatus</italic>
###xml 382 384 382 384 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B78">78</xref>
###xml 475 477 475 477 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B79">79</xref>
###xml 646 657 646 657 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N. risticii</italic>
###xml 776 778 776 778 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B80">80</xref>
###xml 897 898 897 898 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">E</italic>
###xml 907 908 907 908 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">e</italic>
###xml 913 915 913 915 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B17">17</xref>
###xml 1029 1031 1026 1028 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B81">81</xref>
###xml 30 41 <span type="species:ncbi:950">N. risticii</span>
###xml 90 101 <span type="species:ncbi:950">N. risticii</span>
###xml 300 311 <span type="species:ncbi:951">N. sennetsu</span>
###xml 313 324 <span type="species:ncbi:950">N. risticii</span>
###xml 329 353 <span type="species:ncbi:104478">Stellantchasmus falcatus</span>
###xml 458 464 <span type="species:ncbi:9796">horses</span>
###xml 646 657 <span type="species:ncbi:950">N. risticii</span>
Computational analysis of the N. risticii genome and recent molecular characterization of N. risticii have identified alternative sets of potential surface proteins, which include one 51-kDa protein (P51), and three strain-specific antigens (SSA). P51 is a specific antigenic protein conserved among N. sennetsu, N. risticii and Stellantchasmus falcatus trematode agent (SF agent) (78). A previous study had shown that P51 is the major antigen recognized in horses with PHF (79). Computational analysis by SignalP indicates that P51 encodes a signal peptide, and an immunofluorescence assay using anti-P51 antibody on fixed but non-permeabilized N. risticii organisms showed a ring-like labeling pattern surrounding the bacteria, supporting bacterial surface exposure of P51 (80). Jaccard-filtered COG analysis indicates that P51 belongs to an ortholog cluster that presents in all Rickettsiales (E-value <1e-5) (17), and computational prediction using PRED-TMBB method () shows 18 transmembrane beta strands in the P51 protein (81). The discrimination score of P51 for beta-barrel proteins is 2.910, lower than the threshold value of 2.965, suggesting that it is a beta-barrel outer membrane protein ().
###end p 89
###begin p 90
###xml 96 107 96 107 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N. risticii</italic>
###xml 219 230 219 230 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N. risticii</italic>
###xml 262 264 262 264 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B15">15</xref>
###xml 265 267 265 267 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B82">82</xref>
###xml 292 295 292 295 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">125</sup>
###xml 325 336 325 336 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N. risticii</italic>
###xml 371 373 371 373 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B83">83</xref>
###xml 447 458 447 458 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N. risticii</italic>
###xml 619 621 619 621 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B15">15</xref>
###xml 622 624 622 624 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B82">82</xref>
###xml 643 654 643 654 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N. risticii</italic>
###xml 786 794 786 794 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="F7">Figure 7</xref>
###xml 841 852 841 852 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N. risticii</italic>
###xml 984 992 984 992 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="F7">Figure 7</xref>
###xml 1028 1030 1028 1030 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B84">84</xref>
###xml 1046 1059 1046 1059 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">Neorickettsia</italic>
###xml 1122 1126 1122 1126 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ssa1</italic>
###xml 1160 1164 1160 1164 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ssa2</italic>
###xml 1186 1190 1186 1190 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ssa3</italic>
###xml 1254 1258 1254 1258 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ssa1</italic>
###xml 1262 1266 1262 1266 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ssa2</italic>
###xml 1304 1308 1304 1308 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ssa1</italic>
###xml 1313 1317 1313 1317 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ssa2</italic>
###xml 1319 1327 1319 1327 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="F7">Figure 7</xref>
###xml 1479 1499 1479 1499 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">Mycoplasma hyorhinis</italic>
###xml 1547 1569 1547 1569 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">Listeria monocytogenes</italic>
###xml 1572 1574 1572 1574 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B85">85</xref>
###xml 1575 1577 1575 1577 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="B86">86</xref>
###xml 1670 1681 1670 1681 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N. risticii</italic>
###xml 96 107 <span type="species:ncbi:950">N. risticii</span>
###xml 219 230 <span type="species:ncbi:950">N. risticii</span>
###xml 245 251 <span type="species:ncbi:9796">horses</span>
###xml 325 336 <span type="species:ncbi:950">N. risticii</span>
###xml 447 458 <span type="species:ncbi:950">N. risticii</span>
###xml 643 654 <span type="species:ncbi:950">N. risticii</span>
###xml 841 852 <span type="species:ncbi:950">N. risticii</span>
###xml 1479 1499 <span type="species:ncbi:2100">Mycoplasma hyorhinis</span>
###xml 1547 1569 <span type="species:ncbi:1639">Listeria monocytogenes</span>
###xml 1670 1681 <span type="species:ncbi:950">N. risticii</span>
Strain-specific antigens (SSAs), which encode proteins at 50- or 85-kDa in different strains of N. risticii, were initially identified during cross-reaction studies between strains 25-D (P50/SSA) and 90-12 (P85/SSA) of N. risticii isolated from horses with PHF (15,82). Surface labeling with 125I suggested that P50/SSA from N. risticii strain 25-D is a surface antigen (83). Furthermore, P50/SSA has been determined to be a protective antigen of N. risticii against homologous challenge; however, the length and number of repeats varied within SSAs of each strain, which is suggested to contribute to vaccine failure (15,82). Analysis of the N. risticii Illinois genome identifies three SSA proteins (NRI_0870, NRI_0871 and NRI_0872), which apparently are arranged in a single operon (Figure 7D). Sequence analysis indicates that the three N. risticii SSAs contain different numbers of intramolecular tandem repeats, which may contribute to the antigenic variation of this pathogen (Figure 7D, colored boxes in the ORF, and ) (84). Alignment of Neorickettsia SSA genes or proteins using Clustal W or blast2seq shows that ssa1 (NRI_0870) is closely related to ssa2 (NRI_0871); however, ssa3 (NRI_0872) only shares a short N-terminal fragment with either ssa1 or ssa2 and belongs to a separate clade from ssa1 and ssa2 (Figure 7E and F). Proteins with tandem repeats play important roles in pathogenesis and pathogen-host interactions, including creating phase variations (Vlp of Mycoplasma hyorhinis) and controlling bacterial motilities (ActA of Listeria monocytogenes) (85,86). In additional to SSA proteins, intragenic tandem repeats are identified in 17 proteins of N. risticii (). Among them, 11 proteins are predicted to localize on bacterial membrane or periplasm.
###end p 90
###begin p 91
###xml 65 71 <span type="species:ncbi:9796">horses</span>
The roles of these putative outer membrane proteins in infecting horses and regulating host immune responses remain to be elucidated; nevertheless, these proteins could be interesting candidates for protective PHF vaccine development.
###end p 91
###begin title 92
CONCLUSIONS
###end title 92
###begin p 93
###xml 36 47 36 47 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N. risticii</italic>
###xml 379 390 379 390 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N. risticii</italic>
###xml 395 406 395 406 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">N. sennetsu</italic>
###xml 36 47 <span type="species:ncbi:950">N. risticii</span>
###xml 379 390 <span type="species:ncbi:950">N. risticii</span>
###xml 395 406 <span type="species:ncbi:951">N. sennetsu</span>
Analysis of the genomic sequence of N. risticii provides the resources necessary for a detailed study of this bacterium within trematodes hosts and for comprehensive understanding of molecular pathogenesis in mammalian hosts. Despite apparent divergent trematode hosts: one encysting within insects in North America and another encysting within fish in Japan and Southeast Asia, N. risticii and N. sennetsu have preserved complete genome synteny, but genome contents are divergent on proteins with unknown functions. These unique hypothetical proteins could be factors that define their host/vector specificities and could be involved in pathogenesis. Surface-exposed proteins and unique sets of proteins involved in pathogenesis identified in the present study provide valuable insights into future research on PHF vaccines and development of novel therapeutic modalities.
###end p 93
